The Old Canoe

From St. Charles City Republican Intelligencer

1860-03-01

**THE OLD CANOE.**

**BY EMILY R. PAGE.**

Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep;
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro',
Lies at its mooring, the old canoe.

The useless paddles are idly dropped,
Like a sea bird's wings that the storm hath lopped,
And crossed on the railing one o'er one,
Like folded hands when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between,
The spider stretches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl with his dull "too-hoo,"
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.

The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,
Rots slowly away, in its living grave;
And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decay,
Hiding the mouldering dust away,
Like the hand that plants o'er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;
While many a blossom of loveliest hue
Springs up o'er the stern of the old canoe.

The current waters are dead and still,
But the light winds play with the boat at will;
And lazily in and out again,
It floats the length of the rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime;
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew
By the dipping bow of the old canoe.

Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,
And laughed as I leaned o'er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide,
To see that the faces and boats were two,
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.

But now as I lean o'er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver grown,
And the laugh that I hear has a sobered tone,
And the hand that lent to the light skiff wings
Have grown familiar with sterner things;
But I love to think of the hours that flew,
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew,
O'er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

Bradford, Vt.

O’er the moulderiug stern of the old canoe.

From The liberator.

1859-10-21 · Boston, Mass.


THE OLD CANOE.

BY MISS EMILY 3B. PAGE.
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep,
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through—
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.

The useless paddies are idly dropped,

Like a sea-bird’s wing that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing one o’er one,

Like the folded hands when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between,

The spider stretches his silvery screen,

And the solemn ow), with his dull ‘ too-hoo,’
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.

The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,

Rots slowly away in its living grave,

And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,
Hiding the mouldering dust away,

Like the hand that piants o’er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower ;

While many a blossom of loveliest hue

Springs up o'er the stern of old canoe.

The currentless waters are dead and still—
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again,

It floats the length of its rusty chain,

Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime ;
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,
By the Gripping bow of the old cance.

O, many a time, with a careless hand,

I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,

And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—
Where the whirls are wild, and the eddies are thick—
And laughed as I leaned o'er the rocking side,

And looked below in the broken tide,

To see that the faces and boats were two,

That were mirrored back from the old canoe.

But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver grown,
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,
Have grown familiar with sterner things.
But I love to think of the hours that flew,
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe

THE OLD CANOE.

KANSAS STATE GAZETTE.

When? the rocks are gray and the shore Is steep,
And the waters below look datk and deep;
Where the rugged pine, In Its lonely pride,
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding hank;
Where the shadow Is heavy the whole day through,
Lays at Its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddles are Idly dropped,
Like sea-bird's wings that the storm hath topped,
And crossed on the railing, one o'er one,
Like folded hands when the work Is done;
While busily back and forth between,
Tlie spider stictches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl with his dull "too hoo
Settles down on the side of the old emtio.
The stern, half sunk In tho slimy wave,
Rots slowly away In its living grave,
And thegteen moss creeps o'er its dull decay,

Like the hand Hint plants oer the toomba flower,
Or the Ivy that mantles the fallen tower;
While ninny a MnpKom of lively hue
Splines up o'er the stern of the old ennoe.
The current lesa waters aro dead and still­

And lazllv In and out again,
It floats tho length of its rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That in.'ct and part at the noontide chime;
And the shore Is kissed at each turn anew,
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.
O, many a time with a careless hand
1 have pulled it away from the pebbly strand,
Aii padttb'd it down where the si ream runs quick.
Where tlie whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,

Where the rocks arc gray and tka abort la steep,

Wbei e the rugged pine, in it* lonely pride,

Hiding the mouldering dust away,

Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.

it floats the length of its rusty chain,
like the weary march of the hand of time,

Have grown familiar with sterner things:
But I love to think of the hours that Hew
As 2 rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,

Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark ai d deep ; - -j-Where
the rugged pine, in its mnely pride,
Leans gloomigly OTer the murky tide ;
Where the reeds and the rushes are tall and rank, '
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;
Where the shadow is heary the whole day through,
Lies at its moorings the old canoe. '
The useless paddles are idly dropped, ' ' ' "
Xike sea-bird's wing that the wind.haih.lop'd, w.
And cressea on, tbe railing one o wr oae,
Like folded hands when the work is done ; ' r
While busily back and forth between, ,
The spider stretches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl, with his dull "toe hoo,"
Settles do an on the aide of the old canoe. ,
Tbej stern, half sunk in the slimy ware,
Rots slowly away in its living grave,
And the green moss creeps o'er its full d 'csy
Hiding its smouldering dust away,
Like the hand that pJats"cr the tomb a flower
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen rower j
While may a blossom of liveliest hue.
Springs up o'er the stern 0f the old cano
O, many a time with a careless hand
I have puled it away rrom the pebbly strand,
Aad paddled it down where the stream runs quick
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,

To see that the faces and forms were two
That were mirrored back from the eld cahoe. .
But tro'w, as I lean o'er the crumbling side,

The Old Canoe.
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep ;
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride.
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;
Where the shadaw is heavy the whole day through
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddles are idly dropped,
Like a sea-bird's wings that the storm hath lopped,

And crossed on the railing, one o'er one,
Like folded hands when the work is done ;
While busily back and forth between,
The spider stretches hia silvery screen,
And the solemn owl, with his dull "too-hoo,"
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,
Rota slowly away in its living grave,
And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decay,
Hiding the mouldering dust away,
Like the hand that plants o'er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;
While many a blossom of liveliest hue
Springs up o'er the stern of the old canoe.
The currentless wafers are dead and still
But the light winds play with the boat at will.
And lazily in and out again.
It floats the length of its rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands of Time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime,
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew.
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.
0, many a time with a careless hand
1 have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick.
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,
And laughed as I leaned o'er its rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide,
To see that the faces and bouts were two. .
Bnt now, as I lean o'er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver grown,
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone.
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings.
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;
But I love to think of the hours that flew,
As I rocked where tlie whirls their wild spray
threw,
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew
O'er the mouldering stern of the old canoe

From The weekly union record.

1865-07-08 · Oroville, Calif.



Wbete the rock* are pray and the slope U sleep.
And the waters below look dark and deep;
Where the ragged pine in its lonely pride.
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reek and rushes are tall and rank.
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow la heavy the whole day through.
Lay at its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddles are idly dropped.
Like a seabird's wings that the storm had lopped,
And crossed on the railings, one o’er one.
Like folded hands when the work ia done:
While busily back and forth between,
The spider stretches her silver screen.
And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘too-hoo,”
Settles down oa the side of the old canoe.
0. many a time, when a careless hand,
I hare pushed it away from the pebbly strand.
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,
Where the whirles are wild and the eddies are thick.
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide,
To see that the faces and boat* were two,
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.
But now as I lean o'er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver grown.
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,
Have grown familiar with sterner things;
But I luve to think of the hours that flew,
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw.
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew
O'er the mouldering stern of the old cauoe.

Where the rocks are gray and the slope is sleep.
And the waters below look dark and deep ;
Where the ragged pine, in its lonely pride,
T.cans gloomy over the murky tide :
Where the reeds and rushes are tall tnd rank,

And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,
Like folded hands when the work is done;
While busily hack and forth between,
The spider stretches her silver screen,
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.
0, many a time, with a careless ’ and,
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand)
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick.
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide,
To see that the faces and boats were two.
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.
But now as I loan o'er the crumbling side,
And lock below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver gro.rn,
And the laugh that I hear lias a soberer tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,
Have grown f,miliar with sterner things ;

As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw,
Kre the blossoms waved or the green grass grew,
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe

place in our letters :
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
' And the waters below look dark and deep,
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.
The uselesss paddles are idly dropped,
Like a sea bird's wings that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o'er one,
Like the folded hands when the work is done ;
While busily back and forth between,
The spider stretches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl, with his dull "too-hoo,"
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,
Rots slowly away in its living grave,
And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decay,
Hiding its mouldering dust away,
Like the hand that plants o'er the tonib a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;
While many a blossom of the loveliest hue,
Springs up o'er the stern of the old canoe.
The currentless waves are dead and still—
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again
It floats the length of its rusty chain,
Like the wenry march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime.
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.
O, many a time, with a careless hand,
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand ;
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,
And laughed as I leaned o'er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide,
To see that the faces and boats were two,
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.
And now, as I lean o'er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver grown,
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skill' wings,
Have grown familial- with sterner things.
But I love to think of the hours that flew
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew,
O'er the mouldering stem of the old canoe

THE OLD CANOE.
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is
steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep,
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank
And the weeds grow thick on thewindingbank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day
through
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddles are idly dropped,
Like a sea bird's wings that the storm has
looped.

cay,

Like the hand that plants o'er the tomb a
flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;
While many a blossom of loveliest hue.
Springs up o'er the stem of the old canoe.
The currentles3 waves are dead and still-
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again
It floats the length of the rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meets and part at the noontide chime,
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,
j3y the dripping bow of the old canoe.

The Old Canoe.
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep :
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro'
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddles are idly dropped,
Like a sea-bird's wings that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o'er one,
Like folded hands when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between,
The spider stretches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl, with his dull "too-hoo,"
Nestles down on the side of the old can«e.
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,
Rots slowly away in its living grave,
And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decay,
Hiding the moldering dust away,
Like the hand that plants o'er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;
While many a blossom of liveliest hue
Springs up o'er the stern ofthe old canoe.
The currentless waters are dead and still—
But the light winds play with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again
It floats the length of its rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime;
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.
Oh, many a time with a careless hand,
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick,
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was
thick;
And laughed as I leaned o'er the rocking side,
And looked below in the brokeji tide,
To see that the faces and boats were two
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.
But now, as I lean o'er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide
The face that I see is graver grown,
And the laugh I heard has a sober tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;
But I love to think of the hours that flew,
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray
threw;
Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew
O'er the moldering stern ofthe old canoe.

THE C1Z) CANOE.
The following little poem is from the pen
of Albert Pike. Wc give it as a sample of
elegant diction and graceful rythin :
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep.
And the waters below look dark and deep.
Where the rugged rine in its lonely pride
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes ars tall and rank,
Where the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through.
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.
Tre useless paddles are idly dropped.
Like a sea-bird's w ings that the storm bath stopped.
And crossed on the railing, one o'er one.
Like folded hands when tha work is done ;
While busily back and forth between,
The spider stretches his silver screen.
And the sclemn owl, with his dull " too-whoo,"
Restless down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sank in the imy wave.
Hots slowly awoj in its hidden grave ;
And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decay.
Hiding the mouldering pact away,
Like the hand tuat plants o'er the tomb a Cower,
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower,
While many a blossom of liveliest hue
Springs up o'er the stern of the old canoe.
The currentless waves are dead and still,
But the light winds play with the boat at will
And lazily in and out again.
It floats the length of iu rusty chain,
Like the veary march of the hands of time,
That meet aud part at the ntontide chime,
And the shcre is kissed at each turn anew
By the dipping bow of the old cauoe.
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,
I have pushed it away frm the pebbly strand
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick,
When the whirls ere wild and the storm was thick
And laughed, as I leaned on the rocking Bide,
And lovkei1 below in the broken tide.
To see that the faces aud boats were two,
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.
But now, as I lean on the crumbling side
And iouk oelow in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see is graver grown,
And the laugh that I hear has a Sober tone.
And the hand that leut to the light skiff wings,
lias grown familiar with sterner things.
But 1 love to think of the hours that flew.
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw,
Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew.
O'er the mouldering stern of the old canoe

The spider stretches his silver screen ;
And the solemn owl with his dull “too whoo,”
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,
Rots slowly away in its hidden grave :
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,
Hiding the mouldering past away.
Like the hand that plauts o'er the tomb a flower,
Or the Ivy that mantles a falling tower;
While many a blossom of liveliest hue
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.
The currentless waves are dead and still.
But the light winds play with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again
It floats the length of its rusty chain.
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet ami part at the noontide chime;
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew
By the dipping bow of the old canoe.
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick.
When the whirls were wild ami the storm was thick ;
And laughed as I leaned on the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide.
To see that the faces an'd boats were two,
That rye re mirretk-d back from the old canoe.
But now. as I lean on the crumbling side
And look below in the sluggish tide,
Tihe face that I see Is graver grown.
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone.
And the ham! that leant to the light skiff wings,
Has grown familiar with sterner things;
But 1 love to think of the hours that flew,
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray flew,
Kre the blossoms moved or the green grass grew
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe

Where the rocks are gray and the sohea is steep,
And the water below looks dark and deep.
Where the rugged plas to Its lonely pride,
Leans gloomily over the murky tMe;
Where the reeds sad rushes are long and rank,
And the weeds grow thick ata the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddies are Idly dropped,
Like a sea-bird's wings that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing one o'er one,
Like the folded hands when the work is dens;
While busily back and forth between,
The spider stretches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl with his doll "too-hoo4"
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half soak in the slimy wave,
RWts slowly away to its living grave;
And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decsy,
Hliding Its moulderlig dust away
Like the hand that plants o'er the tomb a owver,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;
While many a blossom of levellest has
Springs up o'er the stern of the old canoe.
Tbacaurentless waters are deed and st0il­
Set the light wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily In and out again
It hoats the length of the rusty ehala.
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime;
And the shore Is kissed at each turning maew,
By the dripping bow of the old esane.

STAUNTON, VA., TUESDAY, AUGUST 26, 1873.


.Yoetry.
The Old Canoe.
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep.
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,
Leans gloomly over the murky tide ;
Where the reads and rushes are long and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day
through,
There lies at its moorings the old.canoe.
The useless piddles are idly dropped,
Like a sea-bird's wings that the storm has lopp­
ed,
And crossed on the railing, one o'er one,
Like the folded hands when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between
The spider stretches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl, with his dull "too hoo,"
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,
Rots slowly away in its living grave,
And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decay,
Hiding its mouldering dust away,
Like the hand that plants o'er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;
While many a blossom of loveliest hue
Springs up o'er the stern of the old canoe.
The currentless waters are dead and still—
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again
It floats the length of the rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime,
And the shore i 3 kissed at each turning anew,
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs
quick,
Where tho whirls are wild and the eddies are
thick,
And laughed as I leaned o'er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide,
To see that the faces end boats were two,
That were mirrored b.ick from the old canoe.
But now, as I lean o'er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver grown,
And the laugh that 1 hear has a soberer tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wiugs
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;
But 1 love to think of the hours that sped
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray
shed,
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew,
O'er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

From Forest and Stream; A Journal of Outdoor Life, Travel, Nature Study, Shooting, Fishing, Yachting (1873-1930)

1873-09-11 · New York


THE OLD CANOE.
Sn a :
HERE the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And the rugged pine, in its lonely pride.
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
There lies at its mooring the old ‘canoe.

The useless paddles are idly dropped,
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing, onc o'er one,

Like the folded hands when the work is done:
While busily back ana forth between

The spider watches his silver screen,

And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘* too-hoo,”
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.

The stern half sunk in the slimy wave.

Rots slowly away in its living grave

And the green mass creeps o’er its dull decay:
Hiding its mouldering dust away,

Like the hand that plants o’er the tome’a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower:
While many a blossom of lovelest hue

Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.

The currentless waters are dead and still—
But the light winds play with the boat at will.
And lazily in and out again

It fioats the length of the rusty chain,

Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime,
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.

Oh, many a time, with a careless’ hand,

I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs qnick,
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick.
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side.

And looked below in the broken tide,

To see that the faces and boats were two,

That were mirrored back from the old canoe.

But now, as I lean o'er the crumbling side,

And look below in the sluggish tide,

The face that I see there is graver grown,

And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,

And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,

Have grown familiar with sterner things,

But I love to think of the hours that sped

As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,

Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew

O’ef the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

The Old Cango.

VOLUME XII.

THE OLD CANOE.
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And (he waters below iook dark and deep,
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddles are idly dropped,
Like a seabird’s wings that the storm' has lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,
Like the f lded hands when the work is done;
While busily hack and forth.between,
The spiflcr stretches hie silvery • reen,
And thsofainni>h4; with his dull ’•■too-boo,”
Kettles down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,
Hots slowly away in its livitig grave,
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,
Hiding 1 s moldering dust away—
Like the hand that, plants o’er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;
While many a blossom of loveliest lme
Springs up o’er the tjteru of the old oanoe.
The currenllcss waters are dead and still—
lint the light wind pluys wiih the lioat at will,
And lazily in and out again,
Jt floats the length, of the-rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That mOel and part at the noon-tide chime,
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,
Isy the dripping bow of the old canoe.
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,
i have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side—
And looked below in the broken tide—
To see that the faces and boats were two,
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.
lint now, ns I lean o’er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
Tin: face that i see there is graver grown,
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skill wrings
Have grown familiar with sterner things;
But I love to think of the hours that sped,
As 1 rocked where the whirls their white spray
shed.
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

From Watertown republican.

1873-10-01 · Watertown, Wis.


Where the rock* are gray and the shore is steep.
And the waters below look dark and deep,
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride
Leans gloomily ovc r the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank.
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through.
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddles arc idly dropped,
Like'a seabird’s wings that the storm has lopped.
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,
lake the folded hands when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between,
The spider stretches hie silvery screen,
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave.
Rots slowly away in its living grave.
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,
Hiding its moldering dust away—
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;
While many a blossom of lo\e!iest hue
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.
The currcnticss waters are dead and still—
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again,
It floats the length of the rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noon-tide chime,
And the shore is kisser! at each turning anew,
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.
Oh. many a time, with a careless hand,
1 have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick.
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side—
And looked below in the broken tide—
To see that the faces and boats were two,
That were mirrored hack from the old canoe.
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver grown.
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,
And the hands that lent to the lisht skiif wings
Have grown familiar with sterner things;
But I love to think of the hours that sped,
As I rocked w here the whirls their white spray
shed,
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grewr
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe

From Forest and Stream; A Journal of Outdoor Life, Travel, Nature Study, Shooting, Fishing, Yachting (1873-1930)

1873-10-16 · New York




The well-worn tails are idly dropped,

Like a sea-bird’s wing that the storm hath stopped,
And down from the shoulders, one by one,

Hang the threadbare arms whose work is done;
While busily back and forth between

The spiders stretch their silver screen,

And their struggling victims wearily tote

Across the breast of the old dress-coat.































The glossy cloth that a luster gave

Rots slowly away to its hidden grave;

The green mildew creeps o’er its decay,

Hiding the moldering past away

Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower,

While fading colors, once remote,

Throw their somber hues o’er the old dress-coat.

















The heaving breasts are dead and still,

But the light winds play with the flaps at will;

And lazily swinging its shabby tail

It floats its length from a rusty nail,

Like a pendulum moving the hands of time,

That meet and part at the noon-tide chime,

And the arms together kindly float,

THE OLD CANOE.
Where the rocks are gray and the shoie is steep.
Si d the wan rs below look dark and deen.
Where the lugged pine, in its lonely pride,
Leans gli crnil) over the murky tide;
Where the reeds ana rushes are long and rank,
And the weeds grow thick cn the winding hank;
Where tbe shadow is heavy the whole day
through,
There liea^tt its moorings the old cauoe.
The useless paddles are idly dropped,
Like a sea bird's wir gs that the storm has lopped.
Ai d crossed on the railing, one o'er one.
L'ke the loided hands when the work is done;
WLile busily back and foithbetween
T he spider stretches his silvery screen.
Aid the solemn owl. with bis dull "too-hoo,"
Stt les down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sunk in tbe slimy wave,
Rots slowly away iu its living grave.
And tbe gieen moss creeps o'er its dull decay
Hiding its smoldering dust away,
Like tbe hand that plants o'er the tomb a flower
Oi the ivy that mantles the falling tower;
While many a blossom of loveliest hue
bpiings up o'er the stern of the old caaoe.
The currentless waters are dead and still—
But the light wind piayB with tho boat at will.
And lazily in and out agam
ll Heats the length of the ru6ty chain.
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
that meet and part at the noontide chime,
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew
By the dripping buw ot the old canoe
Oh many a time, with a careless hand,
I have pushed it away fiom the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs thick.
Where the whirls are wild and the eadiesare
thick.
And 'aughed as I leaned o'er the rocking 6ide.
And looked below in the broken tide,
To see that the faces and boats were two,
That were mirrertd hack from tie old canoe.
But now, as I lean o'er the crumbling side,
Aid took below iu tho sluggish tide.
Tbe face that I see there is graver grown.
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wiuga
Have grown familiar with sterner things.
But I love to think of the hours tha' sped
As I rocked where the whitls their white spray
shed.
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew

Where the rocks are gray and the shore Is stcop'
And the waters below'look dark and deep, :

Where the rugged pine In his lofty pride

Leans gloomily over the murky tide;

Where the reeds and rashes are long and rank.
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro,'

There lies at Its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddles are idly dropped, -

Like a sea-bird's wings that the storm has lop­
ped.And crossed on the railing, one o'er one
Like the foldi.d hands, when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between

The spider stretches his silvery screen.

And the solemn owl with his dull too-woo."

Settles down on the side ef the old canoe.
The stern, half-sank, in the slimy wave.

Hots slowly away in its living grave, ;
And the green moss creeps over Its doll decay,
Hiding its mouldering dust away, ' " ' ,

Like the hand that plants o'er a tomb a flower.
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;

While many a blossom of lovktest hue
Springs np o'er the stern of the old canoe. .
The currentless waters are dead and still;
But the light wind plays with the boat at will. .
And lazily In and out again . ..
It floats the length of the rusty chain.
Like the weary march of the hands of time,

That meet and part at the nonetide chime.

And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.
Oh, many a time, with a careless band.
I have pushed it way from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs
quick,
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are
thick.
And laughed as I leaned o'er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide.
To see that the faces and boats were two,
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.
Cut, now, as I lean o'er the crumbling side.
And look below in the sluggish tide.
The face that I sec there is graver grown.
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone.
And the hands that lent to the light skiffs wings
Have grown familiar with sterner things .
But 1 love to think of the hours that sped
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray
shed,
Ire the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew
O'er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

The Old Cange

From St. Louis globe-Democrat.

1876-03-19 · St. Louis [Mo.]

[This text is not available under an open license.]

From The Albion, A Journal of News, Politics and Literature (1822-1876)

1876-04-01 · New York



Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And tke waters below look dark and deep,

Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,’

Leans gloomily over the murky tide;

Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.

The useless paddles are idly dropped,

Like a sea bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,

Like the folded hands when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between

The spider stretches his silvery screen,

And the solemn owl with the dull “too whoo,”,
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.

The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,

Rots slowly away in its living grave,

And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decay
Hiding its mouldering dust away,

Like the hand that plants o'er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;

While many a blossom of loveliest hue

Springs up o'er the stern of the old canoe,

The currentless waters are dead and atill,

But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again

It floats the length of the rusty chain,

Like the weary march of the hands of time,

That meet and part at the noontide chime,

And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,

By the dripping bow of the old canoe.

Oh, many a time with ceaseless hand,
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,

Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick, —

And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide,

To see that the faces and boats were two,
‘That were mirrored back from the old canoe,

But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,

And look below in the sluggish tide,

The face that I see there is graver grown,

And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,

And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings

Have grown familiar with sterner things;

But I love to think of the hours that sped

As I rocked where the whirls their white spray
shed

Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew

O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

From Rocky Mountain husbandman.

1876-06-08 · Diamond City, Mont.

THE HO0E CIRCLE.
THE OLD CANOE.
ALBERT PIKE.
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep;
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.
The useless paddles are idly dropped,
Like a seabird's wings that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o'er one,
Like the folded hands when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between,
The spider stretches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl with the dull " too-whoo,"
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,
Rots slowly away in its living grave;
And the green moss creeps o'er his dull decay
Hiding its mouldering dust away,
Like the hand that plants o'er a tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;
While many a blossom of lovliest hue
Springs up o'er the stern of the old canoe.
The currentless waters are dead and still,
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again,
It floats the length of its rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noonday chime,
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.
Oh, many a time with ceaseless hand,
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies are thick,
And laughed as I leaned o'er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide,
To see that the faces and boats were two,
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.
But now, as I lean o'er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver grown,
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings
Have grown familar with sterner things;
But I love to think of the hours that sped
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray
shed,
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew
O'er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

From The Woman's Journal 1870-1916

1879-04-05



Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep;
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride

Leans gloomily over the murky tide:

Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.

The useless paddles are idly dropped,

Like a seabird’s wings that the storm hath lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o'er one,

Like folded hands when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between,

The spider stretches his silvery screen,

And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘‘too hoo,”
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.

The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,

Rots elowly away in its living grave,

And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decay,
Hiding the moldering dust away,

Like the hand that plauts o’er the tomb a flower,
Of the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;

While many a blossom of liveliest hue

Springs up o’er the bow of the old canoe.

The currentless waters are dead and still—

But the light winds play with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again,

It floats the length of its rusty chain,

Like the weary march of the bands of time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime,

And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,

By the dripping bow of the old canoe.
Oh, many a time with a careless hand
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick—
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was thick,
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,
And looked below the broken tide,

To see that the faces and boats were two,

That were mirrored back from the old canoe,
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,

And look below in the sluggish tide,

The face that I see is graver grown,

And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,

And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,
Have grown familiar with sterner things.
But I like to think of the hours that flew,
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw
Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe

Where the rocks are gray and the shore is
steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep,
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are lone and rank
And the weeds grow thick on the winding
bank; •
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day
through,
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.
.. ‘ "4' \k I : .

And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”

Rots slowly away in its living grave,

Hiding its mouldering dfist away—
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a
flower, r
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;

Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.

But the light wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again,
It floats the length of the rusty chain,
Like the weary march of the hands o f time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime.
And the shore is kissed a; each turning anew,
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.

quick,

From Current Literature (1888-1912)

1889-03-01 · New York


The Old Canoe—Emily Rebecca Page
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep,
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
Lies at the moorings the old canoe.

The useless paddles are idly dropped,

Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,

Like the folded hands when the work is done,
While busily back and forth between,

The spider stretches his silvery screen ;

And the solemn owl, with his dull “ too-hoo,”
Settles down on the sides of the old canoe.

The stern half-sunk in the slimy wave,

Rots slowly in its living grave,

And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,
Hiding the mouldering dust away,

Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;

While many a blossom of the loveliest hue
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.

The currentless waters are dead and still—
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again,

It floats the length of its rusty chain,

Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noon-tide chime,
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew

By the dripping bow of the old canoe.

Many a time, with careless hand

I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,

And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick—
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,

And looked below in the broken tide,

To see that the faces and boats were two,

That were mirrored back from the old canoe.

But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,

And look below in the sluggish tide,

The face that I see there is graver grown,

And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,

And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;

But I love to think of the hours that flew

As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

From The Louisiana Democrat.

1889-08-14 · Alexandria, La.

?he oauisiala fmocrat.
PUBLISHED EVERY WEDNESDAY.
ALEXANDRIA. LOUISIANA.

THE OLD CANOE.
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is
steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep;
Where the rugged pine, In its lonely pride,
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding
bank;
Where the shadow Is heavy the whole day
through.
There lies at its mooring the old canoe.
The useless paddles are idly dropped,
Like a sea bird's wings that the storm has
lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o'er one,
Like the folded hands when the work is done,
While busily back and forth between
The spider stretches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl with the dull "too-whoe,"
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sunk in the glimy wave,
Rots slowly away on its living grave,
And the green moss creeps o'er its null decay,
Hiding its mouldering days away.
Like the hand that plants o'er the tomb a
flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;
While many a blossom of loveliest hue
Springs up o'er the stern of the old canoe.
The currentless waters are dead and still,
The twilivht wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again
It floats the length of the rusty chain.
Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part with the mountain chime,
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,
By the drippling bough of the old canoe.
Oh! many a time with ceaseless hand
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand.
And paddled it down where the stream runs
quick,
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are
thick,
And laughed as I learned o'er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide,
To see that the laces and boats were two,
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.
But now, as I lean o'er the crumbling side,
And look below in the sluggish tide,
The face that I see there is graver grown,
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,
And the hands that lent to the light skiff
wings,
Have grown familiar with sterner things,
But I love to think of the hours that sped
As I rocked where the whirls their white
spray shed
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass
grew
O'er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

steep,

Ijeans gloomily over the murky tide;
Where the reeds and rushes are long and

And the weeds grow thick on the winding
bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day
through,

The useless paIdles are idly dropped,
Like a sea-bird's wings that the storm hag
lopped,
And crossed on the railing: one o'er one,
Like the folded hands when the work is done,
While busily bark and forth between
The spider stretches his silvery screen,
And the solemn owl with the dull 'too­
whoo,'Settles dov-11 on the side of the old canoe.
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,
Ttots slowly away in its living grave,
And the green moss creeps o'er its dull decay,
Hiding its moldering dust away,
Liike the hand that plants o'er the tomb a

From Frank Leslie's Popular Monthly (1876-1904)

1889-11-01 · New York


THE OLD CANOE.
By ALBERT PIKE,
| WHERE the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,

| And the waters below look dark and deep;

Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,

Leans gloomily over the murky tide;

Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
There lies at its mooring the old canoe,

The useless paddles are idly dropped,

Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,

Like the folded hands when the work is done,
While busily back and forth between

The spider stretches his silvery screen,

And the solemn owl with the dull “ too-whoo,”
Sottles down on the side of the old canoe,

The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,

Rots slowly away in its living grave,

And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,
Hiding its moldering dust away,

Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;

While many a blossom of loveliest hue

Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe,



The currentless waters are dead and still,

Lut the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again

It floats the length of the rusty chain,

Like the weary march of the hands of time,

That meet and part at the noontide chime,

And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,
By the dripping bow of the old canoe,

the stream as they were drawn ashore, and a medical |

man rushed from the crowd and led the way into th

From Frank Leslie's Popular Monthly (1876-1904)

1889-11-01 · New York

pier was purely accidental, and gave him great praise for
the gallant attempt at rescue.

Oh! many a time with ceaseless hand

I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,

And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,
And laughed as I leaned o’er its rocking side,

And looked below in the broken tide,

To see that the faces and boats were two

That were mirrored back from the old canoe,

But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,

And look below in the sluggish tide,

The face that I see there is graver grown,

And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,

And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings
Have grown familiar with sterner things;

But I love to think of the hours that sped

As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe,

From Current Literature (1888-1912)

1897-05-01 · New York



Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,
And the waters below look dark and deep;

Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,

Leans gloomily over the murky tide;

Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.

The useless paddles are idly dropped,

Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,

Like the folded hands when the work is done;
While busily back and forth between

The spider stretches his silvery screen,

And the solemn owl, with his dull “too hoo,”
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.

The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,

Rots slowly away in its living grave,

And the green moss creeps o’er the dull decay
Hiding its mouldering dust away,

Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;

While many a blossom of loveliest hue

Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.

The currentless waters are dead and still,

But the light wind plays with the boat at will,
And lazily in and out again

It floats the length of the rusty chain,

Like the weary march of the hands of time,
That meet and part at the noontide chime;
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.

Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,

I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies thick,
\nd laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,
And looked below in the broken tide

To see that the faces and boat were two

That were mirrored back from the old canoe.

But now as I lean o’er the crumbling side

And look below in the sluggish tide,

The face that I see there is graver grown,

And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,

And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings
Have grown familiar with sterner things,

But I love to think of the hours sped

As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
